The Concept of the Observer

We call it perception. We call it measurement. We call it analysis. But in the end itâs about how we take the world as it is, and derive from it the impression of it that we have in our minds.

We might have thought that we could do science âpurely objectivelyâ without any reference to observers or their nature. But what weâve discovered particularly dramatically in our Physics Project is that the nature of us as observers is critical even in determining the most fundamental laws we attribute to the universe.

But what ultimately does an observerâsay like usâdo? And how can we make a theoretical framework for it? Much as we have a general model for the process of computationâinstantiated by something like a Turing machineâweâd like to have a general model for the process of observation: a general âobserver theoryâ.

Central to what we think of as an observer is the notion that the observer will take the raw complexity of the world and extract from it some reduced representation suitable for a finite mind. There might be zillions of photons impinging on our eyes, but all we extract is the arrangement of objects in a visual scene. Or there might be zillions of gas molecules impinging on a piston, yet all we extract is the overall pressure of the gas.

In the end, we can think of it fundamentally as being about equivalencing. There are immense numbers of different individual configurations for the photons or the gas moleculesâthat are all treated as equivalent by an observer whoâs just picking out the particular features needed for some reduced representation.

Thereâs in a sense a certain duality between computation and observation. In computation oneâs generating new states of a system. In observation, oneâs equivalencing together different states.

That equivalencing must in the end be implemented âunderneathâ by computation. But in observer theory what we want to do is just characterize the equivalencing thatâs achieved. For us as observers it might in practice be all about how our senses work, what our biological or cultural nature isâor what technological devices or structures weâve built. But what makes a coherent concept of observer theory possible is that there seem to be general, abstract characterizations that capture the essence of different kinds of observers.

Itâs not immediately obvious that anything suitable for a finite mind could ever be extracted from the complexity of the world. And indeed the Principle of Computational Equivalence implies that computational irreducibility (and its multicomputational generalization) will be ubiquitous. But within computational irreducibility there must always be slices of computational reducibility. And itâs these slices of reducibility that an observer must try to pick outâand that ultimately make it possible for a finite mind to develop a âuseful narrativeâ about what happens in the world, that allows it to make decisions, predictions, and so on.

How âspecialâ is what an observer does? At its core itâs just about taking a large set of possible inputs, and returning a much smaller set of possible outputs. And certainly thatâs a conceptual idea thatâs appeared in many fields under many different names: a contractive mapping, reduction to canonical form, a classifier, an acceptor, a forgetful functor, evolving to an attractor, extracting statistics, model fitting, lossy compression, projection, phase transitions, renormalization group transformations, and so on. But here we want to think not about whatâs âmathematically describableâ, but instead about what in general is actually implementedâsay by our senses, our measuring devices, or our ways of analyzing things.

At an ultimate level, everything that happens can be thought of as being captured by the ruliadâthe unique object that emerges as the entangled limit of all possible computations. And in a vast generalization of ideas like that our brainsâlike any other material thingâare made of atoms, so too any observer must be embedded as some kind of structure within the ruliad. But a key concept of observer theory is that itâs possible to make conclusions about an observerâs impression of the world just by knowing about the capabilitiesâand assumptionsâof the observer, without knowing in detail what the observer is âlike insideâ.

And so it is, for example, that in our Physics Project we seem to be able to deriveâessentially from the structure of the ruliadâthe core laws of twentieth-century physics (general relativity, quantum mechanics and the Second Law) just on the basis of two features of us as observers: that weâre computationally bounded, and that we believe weâre persistent in time (even though âunderneathâ weâre made of different atoms of space at every successive moment). And we can expect that if we were to include other features of us as observers (for example, that we believe there are persistent objects in the world, or that we believe we have free will) then weâd be able to derive more aspects of the universe as we experience itâor of natural laws we attribute to it.

But the notion of observersâand observer theoryâisnât limited purely to âphysical observersâ. It applies whenever we try to âget an impressionâ of something. And so, for example, we can also operate as âmathematical observersâ, sampling the ruliad to build up conclusions about mathematical laws. Some features of us as physical observersâlike the computational boundedness associated with the finiteness of our mindsâinevitably carry over to us as mathematical observers. But other features do not. But the point of observer theory is to provide a general framework in which we can characterize observersâand then see the consequences of those characterizations for the impressions or conclusions observers will form.ChatGPTChatGPTThe "Observer Theory" presented here is a thought-provoking concept focusing on how observers, like humans, perceive and interpret the world around them. As humans we have senses like sight, hearing, touch, taste, smell and balance. And through our technology we also have access to a few thousand other kinds of measurements. So how basically do all these work?

The vast majority in effect aggregate a large number of small inputs to generate some kind of âaverageâ outputâwhich in the case of measurements is often specified as a (real) number. In a few cases, however, thereâs instead a discrete choice between outputs thatâs made on the basis of whether the total input exceeds a threshold (think: distributed consensus schemes, weighing balances, etc.)

But in all cases whatâs fundamentally happening is that lots of different input configurations are all being equivalencedâor, more operationally, the dynamics of the system essentially make all equivalenced states evolve to the same âattractor stateâ.ChatGPTChatGPTThe operation of observers, as described, involves the process of aggregating numerous small inputs to produce an output, which is typically an average or a discrete choice based on a threshold. This process is fundamental to both human senses and technological measurements. Here's a breakdown of this concept:Aggregation of Inputs: Both human senses and technological instruments operate by collecting a multitude of small inputs. For example, our eyes receive myriad photons, our ears detect numerous sound waves, and sensors in devices measure various forms of data.Generating Outputs: These inputs are then processed to produce an output. This output can be:An average or continuous measure, like the intensity of light or sound, temperature, or pressure.A discrete choice, such as a decision made when a certain threshold is crossed, like in weighing balances or digital switches.Equivalencing of Input States: The concept of equivalencing is key. It means that different input configurations or states are treated as equivalent if they lead to the same output. This is a way of simplifying the vast array of inputs into a more manageable form for interpretation.Evolution to Attractor States: The dynamics of the system (whether biological like the human body or mechanical like a sensor) are such that all equivalenced states evolve towards the same attractor state. In simpler terms, regardless of the specific details of the inputs, the outputs converge to a common state or value that represents them.This operational framework of observers highlights how both biological senses and technological tools are designed to manage and interpret the complexity of information in the environment. By reducing and equivalencing a wide range of inputs, they allow for the formation of a coherent and simplified understanding of the world.YouAs an example, letâs consider measuring the pressure of a gas. There are various ways to do this. But a very direct one is just to have a piston, and see how much force is exerted by the gas on this piston. So where does this force come from? At the lowest level itâs the result of lots of individual molecules bouncing off the surface of the piston, each transferring a tiny amount of momentum to it. If we looked at the piston at an atomic scale, weâd see it temporarily deform from each molecular impact. But the crucial point is that at a large scale the piston moves together, as a single rigid objectâaggregating the effects of all those individual molecular impacts.

But why does it work this way? Essentially itâs because the intermolecular forces inside the piston are much stronger than the forces associated with molecules in the gas. Or, put more abstractly, thereâs more coupling and coherence âinside the observerâ than between the observer and what itâs observing.

We see the same basic pattern over and over again. Thereâs some form of transduction that couples the individual elements of whatâs being observed to the observer. Then âwithin the observerâ thereâs something that in essence aggregates all these small effects. Sometimes that aggregation is âdirectly numericalâ, as in the addition of lots of small momentum transfers. But sometimes itâs instead more explicitly like evolution to one attractor rather than another.

Consider, for example, the case of vision. An array of photons fall on the photoreceptor cells on our retinas, generating electrical signals transmitted through nerve fibers to our brains. Within the brain thereâs then effectively a neural net that evolves to different attractors depending on what oneâs looking at. Most of the time a small change in input image wonât affect what attractor one evolves to. Butâmuch like with a weighing balanceâthereâs an âedgeâ at which even a small change can lead to a different output.ChatGPTChatGPTThe example of measuring gas pressure using a piston illustrates the fundamental principles of how observers, whether in the form of instruments or biological systems, process information. Here's an analysis of this example:Molecular Impacts on the Piston: The pressure exerted by a gas on a piston is the cumulative effect of countless individual gas molecules colliding with the piston's surface. Each molecule transfers a small amount of momentum to the piston.Aggregation at a Larger Scale: While each molecular impact might cause minute deformations at an atomic level, the piston, as a whole, moves as a single, rigid object. This movement is the aggregated result of all the molecular impacts, smoothed out due to the scale difference.Strength of Intermolecular Forces: The reason why the piston can aggregate these effects and move as a single entity is due to the strong intermolecular forces within it. These forces are much stronger than the forces exerted by the gas molecules, creating a distinct separation between the observer (the piston) and the observed (the gas).Pattern of Transduction and Aggregation: This process is a common pattern in observation. There's a transduction element that translates the individual inputs (like molecular impacts) into a form that the observer (like the piston or a human sensory organ) can process. Then, within the observer, these inputs are aggregated into a coherent output.Vision as Another Example: Vision operates on a similar principle. Photons striking the retina are converted into electrical signals. These signals are then processed by neural networks in the brain, evolving towards different 'attractors' based on the visual input. Just like the piston, the brain aggregates these signals into a cohesive visual image.In both cases, the process of observation involves breaking down complex inputs into simpler, aggregated outputs. This allows for a manageable and understandable interpretation of the world, whether it's the pressure of a gas or the image seen by the eye. The key is in how these systems manage to equivalence and aggregate these inputs to produce a coherent output.YouOne can go through lots of different types of sensory systems and measuring devices. But the basic outline seems to always be the same. First, thereâs a coupling between what is being sensed or measured and the thing thatâs doing the sensing or measuring. Quite often that coupling involves transducing from one physical form to anotherâsay from light to electricity, or from force to position. Sometimes then the crucial step of equivalencing different detailed inputs is achieved by simple ânumerical aggregationâ, most often by accumulation of objects (atoms, raindrops, etc.) or physical effects (forces, currents, etc.). But sometimes the equivalencing is instead achieved by a more obviously dynamical process.

It could amount to simple amplification, in which, say, the presence of a small element of input (say an individual particle) âtips overâ some metastable system so that it goes into a certain final state. Or it could be more like a neural net where thereâs a more complicated translation defined by hard-to-describe borders between basins of attraction leading to different attractors.

But, OK, so whatâs the endpoint of a process of observation? Ultimately for us humans itâs an impression created in our minds. Of course that gets into lots of slippery philosophical issues. Yes, each of us has an âinner experienceâ of whatâs going on in our mind. But anything else is ultimately an extrapolation. We make the assumption that other human minds also âsee what we seeâ, but we can never âfeel it from the insideâ.

We can of course make increasingly detailed measurementsâsay of neural activityâto see how similar whatâs going on is between one brain and another. But as soon as thereâs the slightest structuralâor situationalâdifference between the brains, we really canât say exactly how their âimpressionsâ will compare.

But for our purposes in constructing a general âobserver theoryâ weâre basically going to make the assumption (or, in effect, âphilosophical approximationâ) that whenever a system does enough equivalencing, thatâs tantamount to it âacting like an observerâ, because it can then act as a âfront endâ that takes the âincoherent complexity of the worldâ and âcollimates itâ to the point where a mind will derive a definite impression from it.

Of course, thereâs still a lot of subtlety here. There has to be âjust enough equivalencingâ and not too much. For example, if all inputs were always equivalenced to the same output, thereâd be nothing useful observed. And in the end thereâs somehow got to be some kind of match between the compression of input achieved by equivalencing, and the âcapacityâ of the mind thatâs ultimately deriving an impression from it.ChatGPTChatGPTThis detailed explanation outlines the fundamental process of observation across various sensory systems and measuring devices, emphasizing the universal pattern and its implications in the development of a general "observer theory." On the other hand, too little equivalencing can result in an overwhelming and unmanageable complexity.Matching Compression and Mind Capacity: The final subtlety lies in balancing the compression achieved through equivalencing with the mind's capacity to derive meaningful impressions from it. Abstract
The applicability of computational models to the biological world is an active topic of debate. We argue that a useful path forward results from abandoning hard boundaries between categories and adopting an observer-dependent, pragmatic view. Such a view dissolves the contingent dichotomies driven by human cognitive biases (e.g., a tendency to oversimplify) and prior technological limitations in favor of a more continuous view, necessitated by the study of evolution, developmental biology, and intelligent machines. Form and function are tightly entwined in nature, and in some cases, in robotics as well. Thus, efforts to re-shape living systems for biomedical or bioengineering purposes require prediction and control of their function at multiple scales. This is challenging for many reasons, one of which is that living systems perform multiple functions in the same place at the same time. We refer to this as âpolycomputingââthe ability of the same substrate to simultaneously compute different things, and make those computational results available to different observers. This ability is an important way in which living things are a kind of computer, but not the familiar, linear, deterministic kind; rather, living things are computers in the broad sense of their computational materials, as reported in the rapidly growing physical computing literature. We argue that an observer-centered framework for the computations performed by evolved and designed systems will improve the understanding of mesoscale events, as it has already done at quantum and relativistic scales. To develop our understanding of how life performs polycomputing, and how it can be convinced to alter one or more of those functions, we can first create technologies that polycompute and learn how to alter their functions. Here, we review examples of biological and technological polycomputing, and develop the idea that the overloading of different functions on the same hardware is an important design principle that helps to understand and build both evolved and designed systems. Learning to hack existing polycomputing substrates, as well as to evolve and design new ones, will have massive impacts on regenerative medicine, robotics, and computer engineering.

- Observers are systems that interact with some environment or "universe" and generate internal impressions or representations of that universe. 

- As computationally bounded systems, observers cannot track all the detailed complexity of the underlying universe. They must equivalence different configurations or states to simpler internal representations.

- This equivalencing allows observers to form aggregated, average descriptions that are reduced enough to fit within their finite cognitive capacities. It produces simplified narratives or compressed perceptions.

- Examples include how we perceive fluids, spacetime, quantum systems, mathematical/conceptual structures, and language. In each case, the observer reduces complexity.

- For something to be an observer, it must maintain some persistence or identity as it interacts with the environment over time. This usually involves embodiment of computational reducibility.

- Observers also need to be of an appropriate scale - large enough to average complexity but small enough that multiple perspectives are possible. 

- Communication and collective frameworks are important as they allow coherent shared descriptions to form, like languages. 

- Ultimately everything is part of a unified computational universe (the "ruliad"), but observers carve out simplified perceptions through their interactions as computationally bounded systems. Their nature shapes how they sample and construct perceived realities.

In summary, observer theory looks at how computationally limited systems like ourselves construct simplified representations and narratives of complex underlying realities through processes of equivalencing and complexity reduction. Both the nature of the system and the environment shape the resulting perceptions.ChatGPTChatGPTYour summary accurately captures the essence of observer theory based on the provided information. This process involves reducing the complexity of the universe into manageable narratives or perceptions, crucial for understanding various phenomena. The theory also highlights the importance of scale, persistence, and collective frameworks in shaping these perceptions, underlining the influence of both the observer's nature and the environment in constructing these simplified realities.YouObserver theory is highly relevant to artificial intelligence and machine learning. - Any artificial system that interacts with data or the world in order to learn and make predictions can be thought of as an observer based on this framework. 

- Machine learning models necessarily perform equivalencing of inputs through things like feature extraction, dimensionality reduction, etc. This allows them to form compressed representations that can be used for predictions and decision making.

- The nature of the models - their architecture, training methods, optimization objectives - shape how they sample and construct perceptions of their environments, just as it does for biological observers.

- Concepts like compression, complexity reduction, multi-scale processing are central to modern deep learning approaches. Observer theory provides a unified way to think about these mechanisms.

- Ensuring models maintain appropriate persistence and identity over time as their internal parameters change is important for applications. Observer theory can help analyze this.

- Current AI systems are narrow and don't exhibit general properties of natural observers like compositional reasoning. Extending them may require principles drawn from observer theory.

- Understanding observer-based perceptions and fallibilities has implications for interpretability, robustness, and societal impacts of advanced artificial systems. 

- Non-biological intellectual technologies beyond current AI, like advanced universal learning systems, could be usefully analyzed through the lens of observer theory.

So in many ways, artificial systems developed through machine learning and future AI can be fruitfully viewed as artificial observers, and insights from observer theory may help guide and analyze their construction, properties, and societal interactions. - We assume reality can be represented symbolically using discrete concepts in languages, compressing immense complexity. 

- We assume consistency and stability to what we perceive, viewing it as governed by objective, predictable laws rather than randomness.

Morphodynamic work

Thermodynamic orthograde processes are vastly more likely to appear spontaneously in the universe 
than morphodynamic orthograde processes. Correspondingly, examples of spontaneously occurring 
morphodynamic work are rare in comparison to thermodynamic work, and are also easily missed 
because their form is unfamiliar. To help identify them, we can begin by defining our search criteria 
by considering some thermodynamic analogies and disanalogies. 
Any change of state is ultimately a thermodynamic change, but some thermodynamic changes are 
more complex than others. In describing forms of work that are more complex than thermodynamic 
work, we are not implying the existence of some new source of energy or a form of physical change 
that is independent of thermodynamic change, and certainly not a ineffable influence. Higher-order 
forms of work inevitably also involveâand indeed requireâthermodynamic work as well. Itâs just 
that the account of certain processes in thermodynamic terms is too simple, completely ignoring 
whole categories of phenomena that play critical roles in organizing what is occurring. This is 
because thermodynamic descriptions only provide an accounting of the energies involved, and assume 
as given the boundary conditions and structural constraints within which the thermodynamic 
transformations take place. But thermodynamic work can also change these boundary conditions; and 
since these are the essential determinants of the organization of the causal possibilities in a system 
(its orthograde and contragrade organization), this higher level of change is often the most critical 
factor for understanding a systemâs dynamics.

- Orthograde changes are spontaneous changes that occur naturally without external intervention. Contragrade changes are nonspontaneous changes that must be forced through external intervention. 

- Examples of orthograde changes include a sugar cube dissolving in water, or a gas dispersing to fill its container uniformly. Examples of contragrade changes include separating dissolved sugar from water, or compressing a gas nonuniformly.

- The probabilities governing interactions between many components guarantee statistical asymmetries, making orthograde changes very likely and contragrade changes very unlikely as scale increases. 

- Individual molecular collisions causing diffusion are contragrade, but the overall diffusion process is orthograde at a higher scale. 

- Contragrade changes occur through interactions between orthograde processes, where one constrains the other from reaching its maximum. 

- Constraints are defined relative to orthograde maxima, i.e. the point where spontaneous change asymmetry ends. 

- Dissipative systems like Earth maintain contragrade processes by absorbing energy constantly to oppose orthograde tendencies. 

- Life involves numerous contragrade molecular processes maintained against thermodynamic orthograde tendencies. 

- Technology uses orthograde processes to drive contragrade changes, like machines overcoming resistance to change.

So in summary, it provides a useful framework for distinguishing spontaneous from forced changes and how they interact across scales in dynamical systems.ChatGPTChatGPTYour summary of orthograde and contragrade changes from Terrence Deacon's "Incomplete Nature" provides a clear distinction between these two types of changes and their roles in dynamic systems:Orthograde vs. Contragrade Changes: Orthograde changes occur spontaneously and naturally, like a sugar cube dissolving in water. In contrast, contragrade changes require external intervention, such as separating dissolved sugar from water.Statistical Asymmetries and Probabilities: The likelihood of orthograde changes increases with scale due to statistical asymmetries, making contragrade changes less probable without external forces.Scale and Process Dynamics: While individual events (like molecular collisions) might be contragrade, the overall process they contribute to can be orthograde at a larger scale.Role of Constraints: Constraints are crucial in defining contragrade changes, which often occur through interactions with orthograde processes that limit their maximum potential.Dissipative Systems: Systems like Earth maintain contragrade processes by continually absorbing energy, thus opposing natural orthograde tendencies.Life and Thermodynamics: Biological processes often involve contragrade changes sustained against the backdrop of thermodynamic orthograde tendencies.Technology and Change: Technological systems harness orthograde processes to facilitate contragrade changes, overcoming resistance to achieve specific goals.Your summary effectively captures the essence of how these concepts apply to various natural and artificial systems, providing a framework for understanding the interaction between spontaneous and forced changes in dynamic systems.YouIndeed, ultimately we will discover that a fundamental
